# Budget 2026 in focus: the record deficit, the critics, and a check against the numbers

On 24 June 2026 the government of Progressive Bulgaria tabled its first regular budget in the National Assembly ÔÇö and immediately found itself at the centre of the sharpest fiscal dispute in years. Deputy Prime Minister and Finance Minister [Galab Donev](/candidate/mp-5158) presented a framework with a **consolidated-fiscal-programme (đÜđĄđč) deficit of 5.7% of GDP** ÔÇö about **ÔéČ7.2bn** ÔÇö and promised the country would not return below 3% until 2028.[^euronews] The opposition demanded the budget be withdrawn,[^db-ottegli] economists called it "a despairing demonstration of fear" and "zero reform,"[^plovdiv24-yankov] and the comparisons reached all the way back to "the Videnov era."[^fakti-obzor]

This article lines up the **verified numbers** from the official documents, gathers **what the politicians and economists actually say** (with a source for every claim), and then **runs those claims through our [budget simulator](/budget/simulator)** to see which survive the arithmetic and which are mostly rhetoric ÔÇö and finally cost out what it would take to reach 3% as early as this year.

## What the budget actually says

The figures below are from the tabled draft State Budget Law of the Republic of Bulgaria for 2026 (đŚđöđĹđáđĹ-2026) and the Updated Medium-Term Budget Forecast 2026ÔÇô2028 (đÉđíđĹđč), which serves as the law's explanatory memorandum.[^doc]

| Indicator (2026)                  | Value                       | Note                                          |
| --------------------------------- | --------------------------- | --------------------------------------------- |
| Deficit, cash basis (đÜđĄđč)         | **5.7% of GDP ┬Ě ÔéČ7,191.9M** | Ôćĺ 3.8% (2027) Ôćĺ 3.0% (2028)                   |
| Deficit, general government (ESA) | **5.4% of GDP**             | Ôćĺ 3.9% Ôćĺ 3.3%                                 |
| Revenue (đÜđĄđč)                     | ÔéČ49,615.3M (39.6% of GDP)   | vs ÔéČ44,014.7M in 2025                         |
| Expenditure (đÜđĄđč)                 | ÔéČ56,807.2M (45.4% of GDP)   | highest level in the three-year horizon       |
| Nominal GDP                       | ÔéČ125,247M                   | real growth 2.6%, deflator 5.2%               |
| Average inflation (HICP)          | 4.3%                        | 5.2% at year-end                              |
| Government debt                   | ÔéČ37.7bn ┬Ě **30.1% of GDP**  | Ôćĺ 33.2% (2027) Ôćĺ 35.2% (2028)                 |
| Maximum new debt for 2026         | up to **ÔéČ10.1bn**           | incl. up to ÔéČ3.26bn under the SAFE instrument |
| Fiscal reserve (31 Dec 2026)      | Ôëą ÔéČ2.6bn                    | statutory floor                               |

Key to the whole dispute: **5.7% is the cash (đÜđĄđč) measure**, while **5.4% is the ESA 2010 measure** for the general-government sector ÔÇö the one against which the Maastricht 3% is judged and on which the European Commission is opening an excessive-deficit procedure. Both are far above the 3% ceiling.

The inflation spike isn't a Bulgarian phenomenon: the forecast assumes a **rise in oil and gas prices after March 2026**, tied to the military conflict in the Middle East and the blockade of the Strait of Hormuz ÔÇö on the assumption that the conflict is relatively short (to mid-2026), after which prices normalise.[^doc]

## Whose budget is this

This is the **first regular budget of the "Progressive Bulgaria" majority** ÔÇö the party of former president [Rumen Radev](/candidate/mp-5142), who won the [April 2026 election](/?elections=2026_04_19)[^cik] and became prime minister of a regular government, with Galab Donev as deputy PM and finance minister.[^econ-gov] The framework was late because of the political turbulence: the previous draft (December 2025) was repealed, the country ran on a "budget-extension law," and the regular budget only appeared mid-year. That is exactly why the government describes the deficit as **inherited** ÔÇö the result of "years of accelerated expenditure growth without a matching rise in revenue."[^euronews] The same is the European Commission's assessment: large, persistent increases on the expenditure side, financed by short-term rather than structural measures, have produced sustained deficits.[^sofiaglobe]

## The consolidation measures

The government presents 5.7% not as a floor but as an **improvement** ÔÇö "narrowing the projected deficit from 7.4% to 5.7%," if you account for what would happen with no measures.[^euronews] Here is the package (all effects are for 2026, as announced):

**Revenue measures**

- The maximum insurable income (đťđ×đö) rises from **ÔéČ2,112 to ÔéČ2,300** from 1 August ÔÇö effect **+ÔéČ90.8M**.
- Minimum insurance thresholds above the minimum wage ÔÇö **+ÔéČ50.9M**.
- Toll system and vignettes (+30%) from 1 August ÔÇö **+ÔéČ53.3M**.
- Improved collection of social-security (đöđ×đ×) revenue ÔÇö **+ÔéČ100M**; Sofia Airport concession ÔÇö **+ÔéČ50M**; additional collection measures ÔÇö **+ÔéČ200M**; gambling ÔÇö **+ÔéČ100M**; Modernisation Fund ÔÇö **+ÔéČ100M**.
- An accelerated tobacco excise calendar (new rates from 1 August too); a licensing regime and a two-component fee for gambling affiliate operators.

**Expenditure measures**

- A one-off 5% indexation of budget-sector salaries (instead of the automatic mechanism, which would have cost an extra ÔéČ564.7M).
- A **10% cut to wage-and-contribution spending** from 1 September (ÔłĺÔéČ85M in 2026, ÔłĺÔéČ170.7M in 2027) ÔÇö with exemptions for municipalities, the "security" agencies under Art. 69 of the Social Insurance Code, delegated budgets and hospitals.
- Removal of the COVID supplement for newly granted pensions after 1 July 2026 (ÔłĺÔéČ2.9M); a **freeze of the maximum pension** at its 2022 level (ÔéČ1,738.40).
- A party-subsidy **cut from ÔéČ4.09 to ÔéČ3.00 per vote** (ÔłĺÔéČ2.1M); the religious-denomination subsidy from ÔéČ10.2 to ÔëąÔéČ7.7 per vote (ÔłĺÔéČ5M).
- Optimisation of central-budget spending (ÔłĺÔéČ545.5M) and of subsidies to the railways (BDZ, NKZHI) and Bulgarian Posts (ÔłĺÔéČ285.3M); tighter control of social payments (ÔłĺÔéČ200M).
- The **minimum wage is frozen** at its 2026 level until a new mechanism under Art. 244 of the Labour Code is worked out in 2027.
- A phased introduction of **personal social-security contributions** for civil servants and the judiciary (80:20 from 1 August 2026), with compensation so that net income is preserved.

For completeness: 2026 capital expenditure is **ÔéČ9,360.6M** (ÔéČ4,028.9M national + ÔéČ5,331.7M European, incl. the Recovery Plan ÔÇö 2026 is the last big year of the RRF); defence reaches **2.15% of GDP** (ÔéČ2,692.8M) on the path to 3.5% "core" defence by 2035 after the NATO summit in The Hague.[^doc]

## Checking with the budget simulator

Here we run the claims through the [simulator](/budget/simulator) ÔÇö the same engine (`bgTaxPolicy.ts`, `bgFiscalProjection.ts`) behind the public tool, [described in detail here](/articles/2026-06-12-tax-policy-simulator). The results below are from an actual run of the engine, not a back-of-the-envelope estimate.

**1. Raising the đťđ×đö ÔéČ2,112 Ôćĺ ÔéČ2,300 really does yield ~ÔéČ90M ÔÇö but as an annual effect.** The simulator's central estimate is **ÔéČ88.3M** (range ÔéČ57.5ÔÇô114.8M depending on how "heavy" the high-income tail is; "central" means at the middle tail assumption, not an average) ÔÇö right around the Finance Ministry's stated **ÔéČ90.8M**. But the catch is timing: the measure only starts on 1 August, so the actual 2026 cash is barely ~5/12 of that ÔÇö **about ÔéČ37M**. Because the Ministry's figure matches our _annual_ estimate, booking it in full as 2026 revenue front-loads it ÔÇö its contribution to this year's deficit is smaller. There is a second catch ÔÇö for the "we won't cut incomes" line: raising the ceiling is in reality a **higher contribution burden** on income between ÔéČ2,112 and ÔéČ2,300 ÔÇö for the worker (less net) and for the employer (higher cost). That is exactly what [Bozhidar Bozhanov](/candidate/mp-5229) of DB means: "from 1 August business is burdened."[^bb]

**2. The party-subsidy cut really is ~ÔéČ2.1M.** ÔéČ1.09 ├Ś 2.861M votes = ÔéČ3.1M on a full-year basis; for the eight months from 30 April that is **ÔéČ2.1M**. Confirmed.

**3. "You can't consolidate to 3% while debt explodes" ÔÇö a claim by Assoc. Prof. Sterio Nozharov (UNWE) ÔÇö is arithmetically false.**[^econbg] Run through the debt-dynamics engine, every **positive** deficit (even a shrinking one) adds debt each year ÔÇö so the deficit can fall while debt rises at the same time. That isn't a paradox; it's the definition of a deficit. The official path shows it plainly: deficit **5.4% Ôćĺ 3.9% Ôćĺ 3.3%**, while debt climbs **32.3% Ôćĺ 33.8% Ôćĺ 35.7%** of GDP. And at 35% of GDP, Bulgarian debt remains **among the 3ÔÇô4 lowest in the EU**. "Explosion" is a strong word, but not an accurate one.

**4. The government itself books a worse deficit than Brussels forecasts.** The simulator's baseline rests on the EC's spring 2026 forecast ÔÇö an ESA deficit of **Ôłĺ4.1%** for 2026. The government, however, books **Ôłĺ5.4%** ÔÇö **1.3 percentage points (about ÔéČ1.63bn) worse**. That's unusual: governments are normally more optimistic than the Commission, here it's the reverse. The reading that the new cabinet is "clearing house" in its first year is supported by [Petar Vitanov](/candidate/mp-5164) (Progressive Bulgaria): "we inherited ÔéČ2.2bn of unpaid, hidden invoices."[^vitanov]

**5. The "soft" revenue measures carry serious risk ÔÇö as the Fiscal Council warns too.** For measures of the "additional collection" (+ÔéČ200M) and "more from gambling" (+ÔéČ100M) type, the simulator applies a realisation factor of 0.40 ÔÇö i.e. it banks only about **ÔéČ80M** and **ÔéČ40M** respectively, not the full amounts. Cutting the BDZ/NKZHI/Posts subsidies by ÔéČ285.3M is ~90% of the entire available subsidy envelope ÔÇö a "hard" cut that large the engine flags as unlikely. This partly confirms the Fiscal Council's worry about **overstated revenue** (in its opinion on the previous framework ÔÇö a shortfall risk of ÔéČ3.5ÔÇô4.7bn).[^fiscal] We also re-priced the entire consolidation package measure by measure: the government books measures worth **~ÔéČ2.46bn**, while our independent count credits **~ÔéČ1.94bn** ÔÇö a gap of about **ÔéČ514M**, coming mainly from the "soft" revenue (banked at 40%), one-off receipts and the mid-year start effect.

In short: **the skeleton of the budget passes the arithmetic check** (deficit, debt, đťđ×đö, subsidies), but **part of the consolidation ÔÇö at least a few hundred million euros of "collection" and "efficiency" ÔÇö is more a promise than secured revenue.**

## What the critics say

The reaction is overwhelmingly critical ÔÇö both in parliament and among economists.

**The opposition.** GERB-SDS, through [Toma Bikov](/candidate/mp-5236), made the historical reference: if the budget passes in this form, "Bulgaria's progress will resemble the mid-1990s, when the BSP and Zhan Videnov governed with a full majority," and called the framework "a sign of a total absence of reform" ÔÇö recalling that Radev himself, as president, had demanded the resignation of the "Zhelyazkov" cabinet over a budget with a 3% deficit.[^bikov] **PP-DB demanded the budget be withdrawn and reworked down to 3%.**[^db-ottegli] [Asen Vasilev](/candidate/mp-5184) (PP) called it "more scandalous" than the budget that toppled the "Zhelyazkov" cabinet;[^db-statement] DB spoke of "the same old budget" with no reforms[^db-statement] and "a whiff of the Videnov era," and [Martin Dimitrov](/candidate/mp-5245) said "a deficit of 5.7% hasn't been seen since Zhan Videnov."[^fakti-obzor] [Kostadin Kostadinov](/candidate/mp-5258) (Vazrazhdane) was categorical: "a complete catastrophe."[^kostadinov]

**Asen Vasilev's figures ÔÇö a check.** The PP leader also made two concrete numerical claims at a press conference that deserve a separate check ÔÇö because they hold up differently.[^vasilev-pc]

The first is about **maintenance/operating costs** (đŞđĚđ┤ĐÇĐŐđÂđ║đ░), which Vasilev illustrates with "electricity, water, telephone, repairs, toilet paper": that they jump 35% (Ôëł ÔéČ1.5bn) and that not spending them would take the deficit down to 4.7%. **The number is right ÔÇö but for the cash "maintenance" line in the đÜđĄđč, not just the utilities he lists.** Appendix 3 of the đÉđíđĹđč shows the whole line rising from **ÔéČ4,251.5M to ÔéČ5,785.9M (2025Ôćĺ2026)** ÔÇö **+ÔéČ1,534M, or +36%**. The governing side disputes exactly this figure: budget-committee chair Konstantin Prodanov points out that on the ESA basis the "intermediate consumption" of the general-government sector rises by **23%, not 35%** ÔÇö from **ÔéČ5,610.4M to ÔéČ6,902.4M** (Table II-6) ÔÇö and that cars aren't maintenance at all but capital spending.[^prodanov] **Both numbers are right ÔÇö they just measure different things:** Vasilev cites the cash đÜđĄđč line (+36%), Prodanov the accrual ESA category (+23%); Vasilev's higher rate is partly an artefact of the low 2025 cash base (deferred payments). Either way it is **genuine national spending**: ÔéČ1.53bn is **1.2% of GDP**, so dropping it lowers the cash deficit from 5.7% to **about 4.5%**. But ÔÇö as Prodanov stresses ÔÇö maintenance also covers **unavoidable operating costs** (hospital consumables, school heating, fuel for ambulances and patrol cars), so not all of it is "waste" that can simply be cut.

The second is about **capital expenditure**: ÔéČ9.3bn planned, a ÔéČ3.5bn increase, "if we don't do them, that's another 3% of the deficit." **His figures are right, but the deficit conclusion misleads.** The sums tie out ÔÇö capital expenditure **ÔéČ5,870.1M (2025 execution) Ôćĺ ÔéČ9,360.6M (2026 plan)**, a difference of **Ôëł ÔéČ3.5bn**. But **57% of that ÔéČ9.3bn is European money** (ÔéČ5,331.7M of European financing, incl. the RRF, against just ÔéČ4,028.9M national), and 2026 is the last big year of the Recovery Plan ÔÇö which is why capital expenditure **falls to ÔéČ6.7bn in 2027.** And most importantly: the European capital programme is **covered by European grants** ÔÇö "grants from abroad" in revenue jump to **ÔéČ5,370.7M** in 2026 ÔÇö so this spending is **deficit-neutral**: don't do it, and you lose the grants too. So "another 3% of the deficit" is a big overstatement ÔÇö only the national financing (ÔéČ4,028.9M) weighs on the deficit, and its increase over 2025 is about ÔéČ1.3bn ÔÇö i.e. close to **1 percentage point, not 3**. He also compares the 2026 plan with 2025 execution; given chronic under-execution of capital spending (2025: just 73%), the real increase will likely be smaller.

**The economists.** Nikola Yankov (Expat Capital): "A despairing demonstration of fear. Zero reform. ShockingÔÇŽ We have no problem in the country ÔÇö no recession, no disasters, no war, no pandemicÔÇŽ Bulgaria deserves a budget with a 3% deficit."[^plovdiv24-yankov] Assoc. Prof. Sterio Nozharov (UNWE): "The budget contains mathematical paradoxes, legal absurdities and contradictionsÔÇŽ Are there any structural reforms and consolidation at all? The direct answer is none." He calls part of the expected revenue growth "disguised inflation revenue" and asks "where is our buffer against the next external shock?"[^econbg] Former deputy PM Nikolay Vasilev called it "the most disappointing, reform-free budget in years," pointing to more than 12,000 vacant administrative posts that no one is cutting.[^safenews-vasilev]

**The Institute for Market Economics (IME)** is more restrained but no less sceptical: its position is that the budget should essentially be "frozen" for predictability and that there is "no deferral" of deficit consolidation; according to chief economist Lachezar Bogdanov, the core unresolved question is how to fund the spending without a serious tax increase.[^ime]

## What the defenders say

**The government.** Donev sticks to the message "**we will not strip away rights and we will not cut incomes**" and describes 5.7% as "the first step toward financial recovery," targeting 3% in 2028.[^euronews] His definition of the problem is memorable: "**an over-deficit is when your money runs out ten days before payday**."[^mediapool] He also cooled expectations that personal contributions for civil servants would plug the hole: "an over-expectation was createdÔÇŽ but that simply cannot happen" ÔÇö affected workers' net income is preserved through compensation.[^sega]

**The governing majority.** Petar Vitanov (Progressive Bulgaria): "The deficit trajectory was above 7%, we cut it by 2% in four months"; "these administration cuts ÔÇö we're the ones making them; no one had done it before."[^vitanov] Budget-committee chair Konstantin Prodanov defended the framework with the numbers: the "ready" 3%-deficit budget the opposition claims to have inherited is a fiction ÔÇö its revenue (ÔéČ50.4bn) was inflated, when 2025 actually collected only ~ÔéČ44bn. He also insists incomes are not "frozen": the minimum wage rises **12.6% to ÔéČ620.20** from 1 January, public-sector salaries by **5%**, and pension spending grows by **~ÔéČ1.2bn** over 2025.[^prodanov]

**Independent voices "for."** The clearest defence from outside government came from Georgi Vuldzhev (EKIP): "The draft budget is a step in the right direction and a significant improvement over what was proposed in December." He thinks the deficit could even be eliminated within a full term ÔÇö but also doubts whether the Finance Ministry team will be ambitious enough.[^econbg] From the BNB Governing Council, Lyubomir Karimanski urged that criticism not be premature and noted that off-budget capital injections (e.g. into the Bulgarian Development Bank) can hide the real picture.[^karimanski]

## Unions, employers, Brussels

**The unions** are split on the specific measures. KNSB voiced "categorical disagreement" with the linear 10% cut to wage spending, but supported raising the đťđ×đö;[^knsb] chief economist Lyuboslav Kostov called the claim that the minimum wage "strains" the budget "economic demagoguery."[^kostov] From Podkrepa, Dimitar Manolov was philosophical: "in a situation like this, no good budget can be offeredÔÇŽ they did what they could," but demanded a review of all 304 administrative structures.[^manolov]

**Employers** are sharper. Vasil Velev (AIKB): "A 5.7% deficit is striking, we haven't had one like itÔÇŽ for several years we've been left on a sled heading for the cliff."[^aikb] (For KRIB, BIA and BCCI we found no explicitly dated statement specifically on this budget ÔÇö so we attribute no position to them.)

**Europe.** On 3 June 2026 ÔÇö only about five months after Bulgaria joined the euro area on 1 January ÔÇö the European Commission recommended opening an excessive-deficit procedure. In its assessment the EC notes that spending has grown mainly on pensions and public-sector wages, financed by "short-term or ad-hoc measures," which raises fiscal risks.[^edp] At the same time the credit ratings held steady: S&P affirmed **BBB+ with a positive outlook** in May 2026.

## Who's right? Claim by claim

| Claim                                                     | Who says it               | Check                                                                                                                   |
| --------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| The deficit is 5.7% of GDP (~ÔéČ7.2bn)                      | Government                | **Confirmed** ÔÇö the arithmetic is consistent                                                                            |
| đťđ×đö ÔéČ2,112ÔćĺÔéČ2,300 yields +ÔéČ90.8M                          | Government                | **Confirmed as an annual effect** ÔÇö central estimate ÔéČ88.3M; 2026 cash ~ÔéČ37M (starts 1 Aug)                             |
| Party-subsidy cut = ÔéČ2.1M                                 | Government                | **Confirmed**                                                                                                           |
| "We won't cut incomes"                                    | Government                | **Partly** ÔÇö true for rates and benefits, but the đťđ×đö and personal contributions do reduce net pay for affected groups  |
| "Consolidating to 3% while debt explodes = contradiction" | Nozharov (UNWE)           | **Refuted** ÔÇö a falling deficit + rising debt is normal; 35% debt is among the lowest in the EU                         |
| "Revenue is overstated"                                   | Fiscal Council            | **Partly confirmed** ÔÇö the "soft" ~ÔéČ600M is high-risk; the model banks ~40%                                             |
| "Zero reform" / no structural change                      | Yankov, Nozharov, Vasilev | **Largely true** ÔÇö the measures are mostly one-off and parametric, not structural                                       |
| "A deficit like Videnov's"                                | PP-DB, GERB               | **Misleading** (see below)                                                                                              |
| The deficit is worse even than the EC's forecast          | Our check                 | **Confirmed** ÔÇö Ôłĺ5.4% vs Ôłĺ4.1% (~ÔéČ1.63bn)                                                                               |
| Maintenance jumps 35% (Ôëł ÔéČ1.5bn)                          | Asen Vasilev (PP)         | **Confirmed (cash basis)** ÔÇö +36% on đÜđĄđč (ÔéČ4,251.5Ôćĺ5,785.9M), but +23% on ESA (Prodanov); real spending Ôćĺ deficit ~4.5% |
| ÔéČ3.5bn capital increase = "3% of deficit"                 | Asen Vasilev (PP)         | **Misleading** ÔÇö figures right, but 57% is EU grants (deficit-neutral); really ~1pp                                     |

The **"Videnov era"** deserves a separate look ÔÇö the most powerful rhetorical figure in the dispute. By size the deficit really is large, and the government itself calls it an "over-deficit." But **the economic analogy doesn't hold**: 1996ÔÇô1997 was hyperinflation (hundreds of percent), a banking and currency crisis, at a time when the currency board was only introduced in July 1997. In 2026 Bulgaria is **in the euro area**, with 4.3% inflation, debt around 30% of GDP (among the lowest in the EU) and an investment-grade rating with a positive outlook. The closer historical parallel is in fact the **2014 banking crisis** (the collapse of Corpbank and the payout of guaranteed deposits), which the critique conveniently passes over.[^corpbank] The Videnov comparison says something about **direction and the absence of reform**, but equating a euro-area, low-debt budget with the 1996 collapse conflates very different phenomena.

## How do you reach 3% as early as 2026? Scenarios with the simulator

The critics want a 3% budget **now**, not in 2028. What does that cost? The simulator gives the precise answer: to drop from 5.7% to 3% this year you need **additional consolidation of about ÔéČ3.43bn** on the cash basis (Ôëł 2.7 percentage points of GDP; Ôëł ÔéČ3.0bn on the ESA basis) ÔÇö and that's **on top of** everything the budget already does. This isn't a tweak; it's a cut the size of the entire annual budget for defence and the interior combined.

Below, each lever is costed with the [simulator](/budget/simulator) ÔÇö on an annual basis and **with the behavioural response included** (which is why the amounts are smaller than the "textbook" ones; raise a rate and the base shrinks). A mid-year start further dampens the effect for 2026 ÔÇö another argument for why the government spreads the consolidation out.

| Lever                                                     | Annual effect (with behaviour)                                |
| --------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------- |
| VAT 20Ôćĺ21% / Ôćĺ22% / Ôćĺ23%                                  | +ÔéČ403 / +ÔéČ799 / **+ÔéČ1,188M**                                  |
| Income tax (flat) 10Ôćĺ11% / Ôćĺ12% / Ôćĺ13%                    | +ÔéČ368 / +ÔéČ734 / **+ÔéČ1,099M**                                  |
| Corporate 10Ôćĺ12% / Ôćĺ15%                                   | +ÔéČ602 / +ÔéČ1,468M                                              |
| Dividend tax 5Ôćĺ10%                                        | +ÔéČ45M (static ÔéČ75M; calibrated to the Fiscal Council ceiling) |
| đťđ×đö ceiling ÔéČ2,300 Ôćĺ ÔéČ2,500 / removed                     | +ÔéČ70 / +ÔéČ693M (over the ÔéČ2,300 already in the budget)         |
| Administration: headcount Ôłĺ10% / Ôłĺ20% / Ôłĺ30%              | +ÔéČ30 / +ÔéČ230 / +ÔéČ430M                                         |
| Budget-sector wages: deepen Ôłĺ10%ÔćĺÔłĺ15% / ÔćĺÔłĺ20%             | +ÔéČ218 / +ÔéČ437M (over the Ôłĺ10% already budgeted)               |
| Pensions: CPI-only indexation (instead of the Swiss rule) | +ÔéČ479M (Ôëł half in 2026 ÔÇö indexation from 1 July)              |
| Capital expenditure (national) Ôłĺ10% / Ôłĺ20%                | +ÔéČ185 / +ÔéČ371M                                                |
| Collection / grey economy (+ÔéČ1bn claimed)                 | +ÔéČ400M (the model banks only 40%)                             |

Three illustrative packages, each of which **closes the gap**:

- **A ┬Ě Purely on revenue.** VAT to 23% (+ÔéČ1,188), income tax to 13% (+ÔéČ1,099), corporate to 12% (+ÔéČ602), dividend 5Ôćĺ10% (+ÔéČ45), plus ~ÔéČ500M of real collection = **Ôëł ÔéČ3.43bn**. The price: every Bulgarian pays more VAT and income tax, and the emblematic 10% flat tax disappears.
- **B ┬Ě Balanced.** VAT to 22% (+ÔéČ799), income tax to 12% (+ÔéČ734), dividend 5Ôćĺ10% (+ÔéČ45), CPI-only pensions (+ÔéČ479), headcount Ôłĺ20% (+ÔéČ230), wages down to Ôłĺ20% (+ÔéČ437), capital Ôłĺ10% (+ÔéČ185), collection (+ÔéČ520) = **Ôëł ÔéČ3.43bn**. The price: spread out, but it touches pensioners, public-sector staff and investment alike.
- **C ┬Ě Maximum on the spending side.** Even if you freeze pensions to inflation (+ÔéČ479), cut the administration by 30% (+ÔéČ430), reduce wages by 20% (+ÔéČ437) and national capital by 20% (+ÔéČ371) ÔÇö the pure spending side delivers **only ~ÔéČ1.7bn**. Add an aggressive collection drive (+ÔéČ600) and you reach ~ÔéČ2.3bn. To reach 3%, **VAT at 23% (+ÔéČ1,188) is still unavoidable**: total Ôëł ÔéČ3.50bn.

The single most contested revenue lever is **removing the đťđ×đö ceiling altogether**: it adds about **ÔéČ693M** (close to a fifth of the gap) on its own ÔÇö but it directly raises employers' costs on higher salaries (and employers traditionally resist lifting the ceiling), which is why we put it in none of the packages above. The milder rise to ÔéČ2,500 yields just ~ÔéČ70M.

And that's the key takeaway: **you don't get to 3% in a single year by "cutting the administration" alone.** The non-exempt budget-sector wage bill that's even amenable to cuts is only ~ÔéČ2.8bn, so even draconian cuts add hundreds of millions, not billions. The arithmetic forces either a **broad tax increase** (VAT/income tax) or **touching pensions** ÔÇö most likely both. That is precisely why spreading the consolidation to 2028 isn't only political timidity: the one-year hit is enormous. But it's also the opposite of an excuse ÔÇö choosing "later" means two more years of deficits and accumulated debt. Open the [simulator](/budget/simulator) and assemble your own package: you'll see just how narrow the path really is.

## In short

The picture is mixed. The budget at least doesn't flatter its starting position ÔÇö it books a deficit **larger even than the European Commission's own forecast**, which can be read two ways: as candour about the inherited imbalances, or as front-loading all the pain into the first year of the term so the later ones look better. Its arithmetic holds up: deficit, debt and the main measures pass the simulator check, and debt stays low by European standards.

But the critics have a substantive point on two counts. **First, the reforms are thin** ÔÇö the measures are mostly one-off (5% indexation, freezes, linear cuts) rather than structural (merging agencies, reviewing spending lines, privatisation / public-private partnerships). **Second, part of the revenue is soft** ÔÇö a few hundred million euros of "collection" and "efficiency" that the execution record suggests rarely materialise in full. Whether the "smooth consolidation" actually happens will only become clear in the 2027 budget, when the cushion of European Recovery-Plan money will also be gone. And much of the dispute itself is less about the facts than about **which measure you cite** ÔÇö cash vs accrual, đÜđĄđč vs ESA.

Every number here can be checked: open the [Budget dashboard](/budget) for the live cash execution and the [simulator](/budget/simulator) to run your own scenarios against the same official figures.

# Following the public money: a citizen's toolkit for procurement

Bulgaria signs tens of thousands of public-procurement contracts a year. The records are public ÔÇö and almost unusable. They live as thousands of files on [data.egov.bg](https://data.egov.bg/) and inside the [đŽđÉđśđí đĽđ×đč](https://app.eop.bg/) system, with no way to ask the questions that matter: *Who keeps winning without competition? Which companies are tied to the people who run the state? Where does my munic├şpio's money go?*

The [Public Procurement](/procurement) module answers those questions. It holds **345,949 contracts and amendments**, from **4,397 contracting authorities** to **26,987 companies**, covering **2011 through 2026**, worth **ÔéČ81.2 billion** ÔÇö and it layers an explainable risk score, money-flow diagrams, a universal search by company, politician or official, a geographic view, sector (CPV) breakdowns, a contracts browser with sector/procedure/value/EU-funding filters, and a red-flag feed on top. This piece sets it against what exists in Bulgaria and abroad, walks through each tool, and ends with what you can actually find with it.

![The module's home ÔÇö a navigation hub with search, tiles into every tool and live scope-aware numbers](/articles/images/procurement-tools/00-hub.png)

*[See it live: Public Procurement Ôćĺ](/procurement)*

## 1. The landscape: Bulgaria and abroad

**In Bulgaria,** the primary source is the Public Procurement Agency (đÉđ×đč) and the đŽđÉđśđí đĽđ×đč platform, republished as open data on [data.egov.bg](https://data.egov.bg/organisation/about/aop). It is comprehensive and authoritative ÔÇö and raw: no analytics, no risk scoring, and crucially no link between a contract and the *people* behind the winning company. In June 2026 the Ministry of Innovation and Digital Transformation launched [đíđśđôđťđÉ](https://sigma.midt.bg) (sigma.midt.bg), a government analytics layer over the *same* đÉđ×đč data. It has a clean interface and ships a working browser of authorities, companies and contracts ÔÇö with filters by sector (CPV), procedure type, year, value and EU funding, search by name, EIK or contract number, sector-level money-flow diagrams, and CSV download of any list (and JSON for a single contract) ÔÇö from day one, a solid base to build on. The more ambitious features it announced (an AI assistant, a green/yellow/red risk index, red-flag signals and a beneficial-owner layer) are, for now, slated for a later stage.

Beyond the official registers, Bulgaria has accumulated civic and commercial efforts around procurement ÔÇö from investigative journalism to business services:

| Platform | What it is | Focus | Status |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [BIRD / Bivol](https://scan.bird.bg/) | investigative journalism + search | PEPs, public spending, companies, procurement, EU funds ÔÇö search by name | active |
| [IME ÔÇö Open Public Procurement](https://ime.bg/) | open-data analysis + a dedicated site (oop.ime.bg) | spending efficiency, indicators | the dedicated site is no longer maintained |
| [Anti-Corruption Fund](https://acf.bg/) | investigative NGO | specific procurement schemes ÔÇö reports, not a database | active |
| [BILI](http://www.bili-bg.org/) | legal-initiatives NGO | integrity, declarations, judicial transparency (not a procurement tool) | active |
| [aop-baza.bg](https://www.aop-baza.bg/), [targove.info](https://www.targove.info/) | commercial alert services | subscription: filtered BG+EU notices, daily email (free trial) | active ┬Ě paid |
| [ZOP Plus](https://zopplus.com/) | specialist magazine + "ZOP+ Assistant" | expert consulting, training, documentation | active ┬Ě paid |

**Abroad,** the reference standard is the [Open Contracting Data Standard](https://standard.open-contracting.org/) (OCDS), which makes procurement comparable across countries. The EU-wide [opentender.eu](https://opentender.eu/) project (DIGIWHIST) pioneered a quantitative **Corruption Risk Index** ÔÇö the work of Mih├íly Fazekas and the [Government Transparency Institute](https://www.govtransparency.eu/) ÔÇö built on objective red flags: single bidding, short tender windows, non-open procedures, supplier concentration. Ukraine pairs its [ProZorro](https://prozorro.gov.ua/) procurement system with the civil-society [DOZORRO](https://dozorro.org/) layer, which monitors risk and lets anyone *follow* a buyer or supplier. Commercial platforms (Spend Network and Tussell in the UK, GovSpend in the US) sell buyer/supplier dashboards and alerts; the US [USAspending.gov](https://www.usaspending.gov/) offers a drill-down explorer with geographic maps.

**Where this module fits:** it borrows the international playbook ÔÇö the explainable Fazekas-style risk index, the DOZORRO "follow an entity" idea, OCDS-grade structure ÔÇö and adds the one thing none of the others localise: the **political-accountability cross-join**. Every contract is checked against the Bulgarian political class ÔÇö MPs, ministers, agency heads, governors, mayors, deputy-mayors and councillors ÔÇö via their declarations to the [Audit Office](https://register.cacbg.bg/) and the [Commerce Registry](https://portal.registryagency.bg/CR/en/Verifications/Verifications). The table below compares feature by feature.

| Feature | đÉđ×đč / đŽđÉđśđí đĽđ×đč | SIGMA | opentender.eu | ProZorro / DOZORRO | Naiasno |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Coverage | all PPA contracts | 2020ÔÇô | EU, incl. BG | Ukraine | BG, 2011ÔÇô2026 |
| Open standard (OCDS) | yes | no | yes | yes | yes |
| Explainable risk index | no | announced | yes | yes | yes |
| Single-bidder flag | no | partial | yes | yes | yes |
| Other red flags | no | announced | yes | yes | yes |
| Money-flow diagrams | no | yes | no | partial | yes |
| Filter by sector / value / procedure | no | yes | yes | yes | yes |
| Links to politicians / owners | no | announced | no | partial | yes |
| Map by region / municipality | no | no | partial | yes | yes |
| Search by person / company | partial | yes | yes | yes | yes |
| Follow / watchlist | no | no | no | yes | yes |
| AI assistant (natural language) | no | announced | no | partial | yes |
| Local-government procurement | no | partial | partial | yes | yes |
| Tenders: forecast vs actual spend | partial | no | partial | partial | yes |
| Appeals before the đÜđŚđÜ review body | no | no | no | partial | yes |
| Connected NGOs (đ«đŤđŁđŽ) | no | no | no | no | yes |
| Sector packs (health, defense, water, farm subsidies) | no | no | no | no | yes |
| Thematic analyses (e.g. roads, ÔéČ/km) | no | no | no | no | yes |
| Access | free ┬Ě state | free ┬Ě state | free ┬Ě academic | free ┬Ě state + civic | free ┬Ě Bulgarian ┬Ě independent |

"Announced" means a feature stated on the roadmap but not yet shipped (as of June 2026).

A second group sits outside this comparison: commercial market-intelligence platforms and public-spending portals. They solve a different problem ÔÇö helping vendors win customers, or making one government's spending visible ÔÇö so they don't score corruption risk or link contracts to politicians:

| Platform | What it is | For whom / focus | Access |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [Spend Network](https://www.spendnetwork.com/) | global procurement database (160+ countries) | suppliers & analysts | paid |
| [Tussell](https://www.tussell.com/) | UK contract & framework data | supplier market intelligence | paid |
| [GovSpend](https://govspend.com/) | US procurement & spend data | selling to the public sector | paid |
| [USAspending.gov](https://www.usaspending.gov/) | official US federal spending portal | government-spending transparency | free ┬Ě state |

## 2. The tools

### An explainable risk index, not a black box

Every contract carries a **Corruption Risk Index** from 0 to 100 ÔÇö the share of the applicable red-flag checks that fired, in the Fazekas/Government-Transparency-Institute tradition. It is deliberately transparent: the page shows *which* checks fired and how many were even applicable ("4 of 8 risk checks"), so you can judge it yourself rather than trust a colour.

![A contract page with the explainable Corruption Risk Index meter and red-flag chips](/articles/images/procurement-tools/01-risk-index.png)

*[See it live: an example contract Ôćĺ](/procurement/contract/701291266900)*

The checks: a **single bidder** (read from the bid count the OCDS feed actually publishes, and suppressed in markets that are structurally single-bid so it doesn't cry wolf[^cpv]), a **non-open procedure**, a **short tender window**, an **amendment** that revises the original deal, a contractor on the đÉđ×đč **debarment register**, a buyer whose spending is **concentrated** on one supplier, a contractor **tied to an MP**, and one **tied to a non-MP public official**.

### Every contract, sortable and searchable

That same risk index sits next to every row of the full table too ÔÇö 340,000+ contracts you can filter by sector, procedure, value and EU funding, sort on any column, and export. The flags show up without opening each contract one by one: sort by risk, or keep only the flagged rows. A summary line above the table tracks the current filter ÔÇö count, total value, share EU-funded and share flagged.

![The Contracts table with its filters, summary line and the risk index next to each row](/articles/images/procurement-tools/09-contracts.png)

*[See it live: all contracts Ôćĺ](/procurement/contracts)*

Scope switches as it does everywhere in the module ÔÇö **this parliament** (only the contracts from the selected National Assembly's term) or **all years**. In "all years" the text search spans the whole period rather than the selected year alone: type a company name and you get its contracts from 2011 to today. Click the subject to open the contract itself; the star saves it to your watchlist.

### Where the money flows

A buyer's spending fans out to its suppliers as a Sankey diagram ÔÇö on every authority page and on the [module's overview](/procurement/overview) page, with companies tied to a parliamentarian highlighted. A company (supplier) page turns the question around: because all its income funnels to a single recipient, it shows instead **how dependent the firm is on one buyer** ÔÇö the top buyer's share, the top-three share and how many buyers it has in all.

![The buyer-dependency lens on a company page](/articles/images/procurement-tools/02-buyer-dependency.png)

*[See it live: a company page Ôćĺ](/company/103267194)*

### The people behind the companies

The module surfaces, on each company page, the politicians tied to it ÔÇö and not only MPs. Mayors, deputy-mayors, councillors, ministers, governors and agency heads are matched to the companies they declared a stake in or appear as an officer of in the Commerce Registry.

![The "political ties" section on a company page](/articles/images/procurement-tools/04-officials.png)

*[See it live: a company page Ôćĺ](/company/202758921)*

Or start from the person: the [universal search on the dashboard](/procurement) takes a politician, official or company and shows the procurement reachable through their connected parties.

![The public money scanner, searching a politician by procurement ties](/articles/images/procurement-tools/07-scanner.png)

*[See it live: connected politicians Ôćĺ](/procurement/mps)*

A link here is a **declared tie from a public register, not an accusation**. Bulgarian law lets officials own shares; it forbids sitting MPs from management roles but not past business or ownership. The tool surfaces the relationship and cites the source ÔÇö the judgement is yours.

### Your own munic├şpio

Local procurement surfaces on every place dashboard: what the munic├şpio, its schools and its hospitals spent, and on whom.

![The local-procurement tile on a place dashboard](/articles/images/procurement-tools/08-myarea.png)

*[See it live: the Plovdiv dashboard Ôćĺ](/governance/PDV22)*

And the [maps](/procurement/by-settlement) show every oblast three ways at once ÔÇö total, **per capita** (so they don't just redraw the population map) and average contract value. Click any oblast on any of the maps to filter the settlement table beneath them.

![Choropleth maps of procurement by oblast ÔÇö total, per capita and average contract value](/articles/images/procurement-tools/05-choropleth.png)

*[See it live: the oblast maps Ôćĺ](/procurement/by-settlement)*

### The red-flag feed

The [red-flag feed](/procurement/flags) collects the signals worth a second look in one place ÔÇö buyers concentrated on a single supplier, active debarments, and the largest politically-connected contractors. And, DOZORRO-style, you can **follow** any buyer, supplier or politician and keep them on your own watchlist.

![The procurement red-flag feed](/articles/images/procurement-tools/06-flags.png)

*[See it live: the red-flag feed Ôćĺ](/procurement/flags)*

## 3. What you can find

**Single-supplier concentration.** Across the corpus, **45% of contracts whose bid count is published had a single bidder** ÔÇö Bulgaria's well-documented competition problem. The feed surfaces the extreme cases: buyers that have sent *100%* of their procurement to one company ÔÇö often small schools and community centres. And sometimes the scale surprises: a secondary [school in the village of Ribnovo](/awarder/000013115) placed a single **ÔéČ2.5 million** contract with one Blagoevgrad firm ÔÇö roughly 95% of everything it has ever awarded. Even if each award was lawful, that degree of concentration warrants closer scrutiny.

**The political class as contractors.** The module records **120 MPs** (current or former) and **82 non-MP officials** tied to contractor companies worth **nearly ÔéČ2.3 billion** over the full period. The largest ties run through state-owned enterprises: an MP registered in the management of the state road-builder [Avtomagistrali](/company/831646048) ÔÇö **ÔéČ842 million** in contracts; a state-agency head registered at the IT company [Informatsionno Obsluzhvane](/company/831641791) at **ÔéČ293 million**; MPs tied to [Bulgarian Posts](/company/121396123) at **ÔéČ186.5 million**. Alongside them are municipal councillors with a directly declared stake ÔÇö e.g. in [Global Cleaner](/company/202758921) (cleaning, ÔéČ27.6 million) and [Etropal Trade](/company/122055819) (medical supplies, ÔéČ15.6 million). None of this is itself wrongdoing ÔÇö it is exactly the map of declared interest that was, until now, scattered across thousands of filings.

**Per-munic├şpio reality.** Because every local buyer is pinned to its settlement, you can read a munic├şpio like [Plovdiv](/procurement/settlement/56784) directly: **ÔéČ4.2 billion** awarded across 17,418 contracts, led by the university hospital, the electricity distributor and the municipality itself.

## 4. Method and limits

All of these features rest on the same open đÉđ×đč / đŽđÉđśđí đĽđ×đč data ÔÇö no private source. The links to politicians add only two further official public registers: the asset-and-interest declarations to the Court of Audit and the Commerce Registry. The single-bidder signal reads the realised bid count the OCDS feed publishes; it is gated against a per-sector competition baseline so structurally single-bid markets aren't falsely flagged. Person links use **only** high-confidence matches ÔÇö a declared stake or a unique-name Commerce-Registry record ÔÇö and are dropped where a common Bulgarian name would create false positives. Out of scope: contracts below the Public Procurement Act thresholds, in-house awards, and foreign suppliers. Coverage runs from 2011 to today, with one gap at the source ÔÇö đÉđ×đč published no usable 2018 contract file, so that year is absent, and 2020ÔÇô2021 carry fewer contracts than the years around them. The risk index is a sorting and screening aid, not legal evidence; every figure links back to its source on data.egov.bg so you can check it yourself.

## Other tools

The module has other tools that extend the picture beyond the signed contract ÔÇö we'll cover them in depth in a separate article. In brief:

- **[Tenders (procedures)](/procurement/tenders)** ÔÇö the tenders themselves, before any contract is signed. We match a procedure's forecast value against the contracts actually awarded under it, so you can see where the estimate diverges from the spend.
- **[Appeals (đÜđŚđÜ)](/procurement/appeals)** ÔÇö the Competition Protection Commission's rulings on challenged tenders, plus an A-to-F risk grade per contracting authority.
- **[NGOs (đ«đŤđŁđŽ)](/procurement/ngos)** ÔÇö associations and foundations: boards, funding, and their contact with public money.
- **[Roads (đÉđčđś)](/sector/roads)** and **[What the state buys](/procurement/sectors)** ÔÇö thematic cuts: road construction with a ÔéČ/km benchmark, and a deeper breakdown by CPV sector.
- **Universal search and connection paths** ÔÇö the dashboard search finds a company, politician or official; on a company page you can check whether a given person is tied to it, even several hops away.

Open the [Public Procurement](/procurement) module, or ask the [assistant](https://ai.electionsbg.com) a question like *"show the procurement red flags"* ÔÇö and follow the money.

## What's new

Since this piece first went out, the module has grown. Here is just what's new ÔÇö the detail lives in the tools above:

- **A navigation hub.** [Public Procurement](/procurement) is now a landing page with one search, tiles into every tool, and live numbers that follow the selected scope.
- **Sector packs.** Whole packs for individual sectors ÔÇö [Health fund (đŁđŚđ×đÜ)](/sector/health), [Defense (đťđ×)](/defense), [Water (đĺđŞđÜ)](/water), [Farm subsidies (đöđĄđŚ)](/subsidies) and [Revenue (đŁđÉđč)](/sector/revenue) ÔÇö alongside the existing [Roads (đÉđčđś)](/sector/roads), with an [all-sectors overview](/governance/sectors).
- **More data.** The corpus grew to 345,949 contracts worth ÔéČ81.2 billion (up from ÔéČ74.1 billion), with fuller 2018 and 2020ÔÇô2021.

The thresholds are recomputed from the corpus on every update. Currently just 1 of 45 divisions qualifies ÔÇö water supply (collected and purified water); neighbouring sectors like electricity/gas/water distribution and postal and telecom services sit just under the threshold.
